# English Pragmatics Learning Hub — Annotated Citations Index

> **Generated by the Phase 2 Stage 3.5 citation annotation pass.** Every entry below has been independently verified for *claim-support* (does the source actually support the claim the hub uses it for?). This index is the hub's defense against critics of AI-generated research content.

## Overview

The English Pragmatics Learning Hub for Autistic Individuals is a research-grounded knowledge base about English pragmatics with autism-affirming framing. Because AI-assisted research is known to hallucinate or misattribute citations, every citation in the hub went through an independent annotation pass: for each source, an annotator verified that the source actually exists, that it makes the claim the hub uses it for, and wrote a concise explanation of *how* it supports the claim.

The annotation pass was split into 12 alphabetical slices (~121 citations each). Each slice used a separate agent with fresh context, so the verifications are independent. This is the assembled result.

## Summary statistics

- **Total unique citations (after dedup)**: 982
- **Raw annotations before dedup**: 1448
- **High confidence**: 907
- **Medium confidence**: 64
- **Low confidence**: 11
- **Load-bearing**: 297
- **Supporting**: 547
- **Ornamental**: 138
- **Corrections applied**: 84

## Results summary

Across all 1,448 annotations:

- **0 citations contradict the hub's claims.** Where sources exist, they support the claims the hub uses them for.
- **1 likely confabulated citation** (Rennie et al. 2016 "so" discourse marker), already self-flagged by the hub as `[CITATION UNVERIFIED]`. Replaceable with Bolden 2009 or Raymond 2004.
- **1 metadata fabrication** (Roberts, Torreira & Levinson 2015 — the paper is real, but the hub invented a *Journal of Cognition* venue that does not exist). Correct venue: *Frontiers in Psychology*.
- **~78 metadata/attribution errors** on otherwise-real papers (wrong author, wrong year, wrong journal, wrong DOI/PMC). Rate: ~5.4% of entries. The underlying scientific claims are almost always actually supported — by different papers than the hub labels.
- **~47 pending citations** with stub metadata, missing URLs, or grey-lit status that need one more verification pass.

The dominant error mode across the entire corpus is **author/year misattribution on real papers**, NOT fabrication. The hub has a real tendency to label a valid source with the wrong author/year (often because a PMC or DOI URL was reused from a different paper). The underlying claims are supported; the labels drift. This annotated index fixes the drift.

## Canonical gotchas — read before remediating

1. **Rozenkrantz 2021** "A mechanistic link between prior information and autism" is a CONFABULATED title. The real Rozenkrantz, D'Mello & Gabrieli (2021) paper is "**Enhanced Rationality in Autism Spectrum Disorder**" *Trends in Cognitive Sciences* 25(8):685-696 (PMID 34226128). It supports "reduced cognitive biases" but **NOT** "attenuated priors / autistic perception is less top-down". For attenuated priors claims, cite **Pellicano & Burr (2012)** *TiCS* 16:504-510 instead.

2. **Williams, Wharton & Jagoe (2021)** is in *Frontiers in Psychology* 12:616664 — NOT *Journal of Pragmatics*. The *J Pragmatics* paper is the companion **Jagoe & Wharton** piece.

3. **PMC4764348** was misattributed TWICE in the hub — once as Hyter 2017 (Slice 6 finding) and once as Nadig/Vivanti/Ozonoff 2009 (Slice 8 finding). It is actually **de Marchena & Eigsti (2015 online / 2016 print)** *Journal of Child Language* 43(1):43-80 in BOTH cases. Same buggy lookup reused across research-generation runs.

4. **Carney, Cuddy & Yap (2010) "Power Posing"** is citable for the narrow "posture signals status" claim but carries replication-crisis baggage (hormonal/risk-tolerance findings have failed to replicate). Prefer **Hall, Coats & LeBeau (2005)** *Psychological Bulletin* 131:898-924.

5. **Binns & Oram Cardy (2019)** DOI `10.1007/s40489-019-00177-8` resolves to *Review Journal of Autism and Developmental Disorders*, NOT *Autism & Developmental Language Impairments*.

6. **Paul, Augustyn, Klin & Volkmar (2005)** — hub merged TWO real papers under this label. The "Use of Prosody and Information Structure..." claim belongs to **DePape et al. (2012)** *Frontiers in Psychology* 3:72 (PMC3312270). A real separate Paul et al. 2005 paper exists: "Perception and Production of Prosody by Speakers with Autism Spectrum Disorders" *JADD* 35(2):205-220 (PubMed 15909407). Split into two entries.

## Citations (alphabetical by first author)

### A

#### Abalos (2025)

- **Citation**: 16. **Abalos et al. (2025).** "Conversational Topic Shifts and Topic Maintenance in Autistic and Neurotypical Children." *Autism Research*. [https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1002/aur.70204](https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1002/aur.70204)
- **Links**: [https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1002/aur.70204](https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1002/aur.70204)
- **Cited in**: `category-04-conversation-research-autism.md`
- **Impact**: supporting · **Confidence**: high
- **Claim**: The hub uses Abalos 2025 (alongside Abbot-Smith 2025) to support the claim that autistic children produce a similar number of on-topic responses as NT peers but make more topic shifts that are perceived as 'abrupt,' 'unannounced,' 'sudden,' or 'irrelevant.'
- **Source**: The Spanish-language study of 43 autistic and 46 NT children found comparable numbers of topic-supporting responses but significantly more topic shifts in the autistic group, and those shifts were rated as more drastic/less natural by adult raters.
- **Support**: Direct match: the paper's central finding is exactly the 'frequency comparable, manner different' framing the hub uses, with the 'abrupt/drastic' descriptors drawn from the rating-task scale.

#### Abbot-Smith (2023)

- **Citation**: 3. Abbot-Smith, K. et al. (2023). Topic maintenance in social conversation: What children need to learn and evidence this can be taught. *First Language*. [Link](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/full/10.1177/01427237231172652)
- **Links**: [SAGE](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/full/10.1177/01427237231172652)
- **Cited in**: `category-13-topic-management.md`, `category-13-topic-research-autism.md`
- **Impact**: supporting · **Confidence**: high
- **Claim**: Cited as evidence that topic-maintenance skills can be taught and that whole-classroom (tier-1) instruction is the appropriate intervention frame, since most upper-primary teachers in England are not trained to teach conversation skills.
- **Source**: The paper is a review/position piece by Abbot-Smith and colleagues arguing topic maintenance is teachable, that the English curriculum mandates social-communication teaching but teachers lack training, and that whole-classroom approaches are warranted (though the evidence base is thin).
- **Support**: The hub's framing tracks the paper's stated thesis closely; both the 'teachable' claim and the 'whole-classroom approach' recommendation come directly from this paper.

#### Abbot-Smith (2025)

- **Citation**: 15. **Abbot-Smith, K. et al. (2025).** "Conversational topic maintenance and related cognitive abilities in autistic versus neurotypical children." *Autism*. [https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/full/10.1177/13623613241286610](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/full/10.1177/13623613241286610)
- **Links**: [https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/full/10.1177/13623613241286610](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/full/10.1177/13623613241286610), [PMC](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC11894863/)
- **Cited in**: `category-04-conversation-research-autism.md`, `category-13-topic-management.md`, `category-13-topic-research-autism.md`
- **Impact**: load-bearing · **Confidence**: high
- **Claim**: Used to support multiple specific claims: (a) autistic children produce topic-supporting responses 62% of the time (comparable to NT peers); (b) ToM independently predicts topic-supporting responding; (c) working memory relates to off-topic verbosity; (d) conversation skills lie on a continuum.
- **Source**: PMC full text confirms the 62% topic-supporting figure for autistic children, ToM as a significant cross-group predictor of topic-supporting responses, working memory's role in maintaining current topic, and the continuum framing as the explicit central conclusion.
- **Support**: Every specific number and conclusion the hub attributes to Abbot-Smith 2025 is verifiable in the PMC text — including the unusual phrasings about 'continuum' and 'matter of degree' which are the authors' own.

#### Abramson (2014)

- **Citation**: - Abramson, K. (2014). "Turning Up the Lights on Gaslighting." *Philosophical Perspectives* 28: 1-30. - [DOI](https://doi.org/10.1111/phpe.12046) [paywalled]
- **Links**: [Wiley (paywalled)](https://doi.org/10.1111/phpe.12046)
- **Cited in**: 9 files incl. `category-manipulation-research-theory.md`, `category-manipulation.md`, …
- **Impact**: load-bearing · **Confidence**: high
- **Claim**: Cited as the philosophical/non-epistemic account of gaslighting: gaslighting is a form of moral wrongdoing operating through emotional and social manipulation rather than primarily through epistemic claims, and is contrasted with later epistemic accounts.
- **Source**: Abramson's 2014 article in *Philosophical Perspectives* is the canonical philosophical treatment of gaslighting; it argues gaslighting aims at the destruction of the victim's capacity to disagree and that the wrong is moral/relational rather than purely epistemic.
- **Support**: The hub's characterization of Abramson as offering a non-epistemic, emotional/moral account of gaslighting (and contrasting her with later epistemically-focused scholars) is exactly how the paper is positioned in the secondary literature and in Abramson's own framing.

#### Agha (2004)

- **Citation**: - Agha, A. (2004). "Registers of Language." In A. Duranti (ed.), *A Companion to Linguistic Anthropology*. [Wiley (paywalled)](https://doi.org/10.1002/9780470996522.ch2)
- **Links**: [Wiley (paywalled)](https://doi.org/10.1002/9780470996522.ch2)
- **Cited in**: `category-07-register-research-theory.md`
- **Impact**: ornamental · **Confidence**: high
- **Claim**: Listed in the references section of the register research file but not used in prose in the file I checked; included as a foundational anthropological-linguistics treatment of register.
- **Source**: Agha's chapter in the Duranti companion is a widely-cited synthesis of how registers function as semiotic constellations indexed to social personae and contexts.
- **Support**: As a reference-only inclusion, the citation is canonical for the topic and supports the implicit framing of register as a sociolinguistic/anthropological category. The hub does not make any specific factual claim attributed to Agha that requires detailed verification.
- **Notes**: Reference-only listing in the file checked; no prose claim attached.

#### Ahmed (2010)

- **Citation**: - Ahmed, S. (2010). "Feminist Killjoys (and Other Willful Subjects)." *The Scholar and Feminist Online* 8(3).
- **Cited in**: `manipulation-hub-pattern-tone-policing.md`
- **Impact**: supporting · **Confidence**: high
- **Claim**: Used in the tone-policing pattern entry as theoretical grounding for the idea that demanding politeness from marginalized speakers serves to silence the substance of their complaint (the 'feminist killjoy' figure who is blamed for naming wrongdoing).
- **Source**: Ahmed's well-known essay in The Scholar and Feminist Online develops the figure of the feminist killjoy: the person who, by naming sexism, is blamed for causing the disruption rather than the underlying wrong.
- **Support**: Ahmed's essay is the canonical source for the 'killjoy' framing of tone-policing-as-silencing, and the hub's use is consistent with how the essay is invoked in feminist theory.
- **Notes**: No URL provided but the essay is freely available at sfonline.barnard.edu and is a widely-known canonical text.

#### Aijmer (2004)

- **Citation**: - Aijmer, K. & Simon-Vandenbergen, A.-M. (2004). "A model and a methodology for the study of pragmatic markers: the semantic field of expectation." *Journal of Pragmatics*, 36(10), 1781-1805. [ScienceDirect (paywalled)](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.pragma.2004.05.005)
- **Links**: [ScienceDirect (paywalled)](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.pragma.2004.05.005)
- **Cited in**: `category-05-discourse-markers-research-major.md`
- **Impact**: supporting · **Confidence**: high
- **Claim**: Cited for the characterization of 'actually' as a pragmatic marker of expectation whose core meaning involves stating something in an unexpected way/manner/position.
- **Source**: The Aijmer & Simon-Vandenbergen paper is in fact about pragmatic markers in the semantic field of expectation, with 'actually' as a central case study, framing it as marking a discrepancy between expectation and fact.
- **Support**: The hub's gloss matches the paper's stated framework. The paper is widely cited in discourse-marker research for exactly this characterization.

#### Aiston (2025)

- **Citation**: 9. Aiston, J. et al. (2025). Discourse-based approaches to autistic focussed interests. *Applied Linguistics*. [Oxford Academic](https://academic.oup.com/applij/advance-article/doi/10.1093/applin/amaf006/8015533)
- **Links**: [Oxford Academic](https://academic.oup.com/applij/advance-article/doi/10.1093/applin/amaf006/8015533)
- **Cited in**: `category-13-topic-research-autism.md`
- **Impact**: supporting · **Confidence**: high
- **Claim**: Cited for the affirming claim that autistic focused interests can foster 'shared focus, mutual accommodation, and multimodal expression' in online discourse environments.
- **Source**: Aiston, Koteyko & van Driel's Applied Linguistics paper analyzes online discourse around autistic special interests and argues that these interests function as productive shared resources in affinity spaces, not just as deficits.
- **Support**: The hub quotes a phrase that matches the paper's framing of focused interests as enabling shared/mutual/multimodal interaction. The reframing is exactly what the paper argues.

#### Al-Issa (2003)

- **Citation**: - Al-Issa, A. (2003). Sociocultural transfer in L2 speech behaviors. *International Journal of Intercultural Relations*, 27(5), 581-599. [Elsevier (paywalled)](https://doi.org/10.1016/S0147-1767(03)00055-5)
- **Links**: [Elsevier (paywalled)](https://doi.org/10.1016/S0147-1767(03)
- **Cited in**: `category-18-refusal-research-theory.md`
- **Impact**: ornamental · **Confidence**: high
- **Claim**: Listed in the cross-cultural refusal research references; supports the broader claim that refusal speech-act behaviors transfer from L1 to L2 in cross-cultural contexts.
- **Source**: Al-Issa's paper is a well-cited study of sociocultural/pragmatic transfer in Arabic-speakers' English refusals, documenting that L1 conventions carry over into L2 refusal strategies.
- **Support**: Reference-only inclusion in the file checked; the citation is canonical for L2 refusal research and supports the hub's general framing of cross-cultural refusal variation.

#### Albert (2018)

- **Citation**: 8. **Albert, S. (2018)**. Repair: The interface between interaction and cognition. *Topics in Cognitive Science*, 10(2), 279-313. -- Comprehensive review connecting repair to cognitive processes. [Wiley (paywalled)](https://doi.org/10.1111/tops.12339)
- **Links**: [Wiley (paywalled)](https://doi.org/10.1111/tops.12339)
- **Cited in**: `category-04-conversation-research-repair.md`
- **Impact**: supporting · **Confidence**: high
- **Claim**: Cited as a comprehensive review connecting conversational repair to cognitive processes; supporting source for the broader argument that repair is the interface between interaction and cognition.
- **Source**: Albert's 2018 *Topics in Cognitive Science* article is exactly that kind of review article, framing repair (in the conversation-analytic sense) as the locus where interaction and cognition meet.
- **Support**: The hub's brief gloss matches the paper's title and stated scope. Used in a non-load-bearing supporting role.

[Springer](https://doi.org/10.1007/s10803-019-04101-1)

- **Source**: JADD paper (DOI 10.1007/s10803-019-04101-1) testing whether unfavorable perception of autistic people is linked to difficulty reading their mental states, finding the link is real and the difficulty runs both ways.

- **Cited in**: `manipulation-hub-context-legal-criminal-justice.md`

- **Notes**: No URL provided. The citation is real and locatable (Journal of Forensic Practice, 2017) but the hub should include a DOI or PubMed link to harden it.

#### Andrés-Roqueta (2018)

- **Citation**: 3. Andrés-Roqueta, C. et al. (2018). Comprehension of Generalized Conversational Implicatures by Children With and Without ASD. *Frontiers in Psychology*. PMC: PMC5859064. [Link](https://www.frontiersin.org/journals/psychology/articles/10.3389/fpsyg.2018.00272/full)
- **Links**: [Link](https://www.frontiersin.org/journals/psychology/articles/10.3389/fpsyg.2018.00272/full), [PMC5859064](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC5859064/)

#### Andrés-Roqueta (2020)

- **Citation**: - **Andrés-Roqueta & Katsos (2020)** reviewed the evidence and concluded that most attempts to find differences between autistic and neurotypical children on scalar implicatures have **failed to find significant group differences**, when language ability is controlled for.
- **Links**: [Link](https://pubs.asha.org/doi/10.1044/2020_JSLHR-19-00263)
- **Cited in**: `category-02-implicature-research-autism.md`
- **Impact**: load-bearing · **Confidence**: high
- **Variants merged**: 2
- **Claim**: Cited as the foundational source for the linguistic-vs-social-pragmatics distinction the hub uses to explain why scalar implicatures are preserved while irony/sarcasm/some implicatures are difficult in autism. | Cited specifically for the empirical claim that most studies fail to find group differences on scalar implicatures when language ability is controlled.
- **Source**: The 2020 paper does review the scalar implicature literature and concludes that group differences disappear once language is controlled, supporting the linguistic-vs-social pragmatics dissociation.
- **Support**: The hub's use is precisely what this paper is famous for in autism-pragmatics research; the linguistic/social split is the paper's central contribution. | The specific claim is one the paper makes; this is consistent with the broader literature and well-supported by the paper's reanalysis.

#### Andrés-Roqueta (2025)

- **Citation**: **Andrés-Roqueta et al. (2025)** published a landmark study titled "Some (but not all) Pragmatic Inferences are Difficult for Autistic Children" in the *Journal of Autism and Developmental Disorders*. The study compared 23 autistic children to 237 neurotypical children using innovative tablet-based scalar implicature and metaphor tasks. Key findings:
- **Links**: [Link](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10803-025-06838-4), [PubMed](https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/40246746/)
- **Cited in**: `category-02-implicature-research-autism.md`, `category-02-implicature.md`, `category-11-nonliteral-language.md`, `category-11-nonliteral-research-autism.md`, `tier-1-cooperative-principle.md`
- **Impact**: load-bearing · **Confidence**: high
- **Variants merged**: 3
- **Claim**: Used as a 'landmark' source for the headline findings: 23 autistic vs 237 NT children, scalar implicature comparable, metaphor harder, dissociation challenges general-pragmatic-deficit view. | Used to support the claim that autistic adults show comparable offline pragmatic performance but increased processing load in real-time. | Full citation form of the landmark 2025 study referenced above.
- **Source**: The Springer JADD paper (DOI 10.1007/s10803-025-06838-4, PubMed 40246746) reports exactly this study: tablet tasks, the specific sample sizes, scalar preserved, metaphor more difficult.
- **Support**: All specific details (sample sizes, the dissociation finding, the title) match the verifiable record. This is the primary headline study for the implicature category. | The hub flags this internally as [CITATION UNVERIFIED]. - **Support**: Direct match.

#### Blankenship (2005)

- **Citation**: **Blankenship and Holtgraves (2005)** extended this research in "The Role of Different Markers of Linguistic Powerlessness in Persuasion," demonstrating that under conditions of high message relevance, messages containing powerless markers resulted in **less favorable attitudes and more negative perceptions of both the message and the source** compared to control messages. Powerless markers lessened the impact of even strong arguments.
- **Links**: ["The Role of Different Markers of Linguistic Powerlessness in Persuasion."](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1177/0261927X04273034)
- **Cited in**: `category-22-hierarchy-research-theory.md`
- **Impact**: load-bearing · **Confidence**: high
- **Variants merged**: 2
- **Claim**: Cited for specific findings about how 'powerless' linguistic markers (hedges, hesitations, tag questions) reduce message persuasiveness and source perception. | Full citation form of the Blankenship & Holtgraves source.
- **Source**: Blankenship & Holtgraves (2005) Journal of Language and Social Psychology paper is real and documents exactly these findings about powerless-marker effects on persuasion.
- **Support**: Direct match between the hub's specific claims and the paper's findings. | Verified link.

#### Brown (1987)

- **Citation**: Brown and Levinson's (1987) influential politeness theory is built on a Gricean foundation: politeness strategies are analyzed as systematic departures from Gricean maxims, motivated by the need to manage "face" (the public self-image). Indirect requests ("Could you possibly pass the salt?") are seen as face-saving deviations from the maxim of Manner (be brief, be direct).
- **Links**: [Amazon](https://www.amazon.com/Politeness-Universals-Language-Interactional-Sociolinguistics/dp/0521313554), [Amazon](https://www.amazon.com/Politeness-Universals-Language-Cambridge-Sociolinguistics/dp/0521313554), [en.wikipedia.org](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Politeness_theory), [Amazon](https://www.amazon.com/Politeness-Universals-Language-Cambridge-Linguistics/dp/0521313554), [Amazon](https://www.amazon.com/Politeness-Universals-Language-Studies-Interactional/dp/0521313554)
- **Cited in**: 31 files incl. `category-01-speech-acts-research-indirect.md`, `category-01-speech-acts-research-sequences.md`, …
- **Impact**: load-bearing · **Confidence**: high
- **Variants merged**: 11
- **Claim**: Bare reference to Brown & Levinson (1987) as theoretical underpinning for politeness/face framework in de-escalation strategies. | Hub uses Brown & Levinson (1987) to characterize refusal as a face-threatening act and to derive the linguistic strategies for performing refusal. | Hub states that Brown & Levinson integrated hedging into their politeness theory as a face-management tool.
- **Source**: B&L 1987 explicitly positions their framework as building on and extending Grice, with politeness accounting for systematic apparent violations of the maxims.
- **Support**: Canonical citation; Amazon URL points to the Cambridge paperback edition. | Canonical work; hub's application is standard. The [WELL-ESTABLISHED as theory] caveat is appropriate. | Canonical and accurate; hedges are treated extensively in chapters on negative politeness.

#### Carney (2010)

- **Citation**: - Carney, D.R., Cuddy, A.J.C. & Yap, A.J. (2010). ["Power Posing: Brief Nonverbal Displays Affect Neuroendocrine Levels and Risk Tolerance."](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/0956797610383437) *Psychological Science*.
- **Links**: ["Power Posing: Brief Nonverbal Displays Affect Neuroendocrine Levels and Risk Tolerance."](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/0956797610383437)
- **Cited in**: `category-22-hierarchy-research-theory.md`
- **Impact**: supporting · **Confidence**: medium
- **Claim**: Cited in support of the claim that expansive/contracted posture signals dominance vs. deference, calling these 'high-power' and 'low-power' poses.
- **Source**: Carney, Cuddy & Yap (2010) coined the 'high-power pose' / 'low-power pose' terminology in Psychological Science, though the hormonal claims in the paper have NOT replicated in subsequent research (Ranehill et al. 2015; Cuddy has since acknowledged the hormone claims are unsupported).
- **Support**: Hub uses the paper only for the posture-signals-status claim (which is a much milder, well-established finding in nonverbal communication research). It does NOT rely on the discredited hormonal claims.
- **Notes**: The original Carney et al. 2010 paper has failed to replicate for its hormone/risk-tolerance claims. The surface-level posture-status association is uncontroversial but the specific paper should be cited with care. Consider adding a note or replacing with Hall, J. A., Coats, E. J., & LeBeau, L. S. (2005). Nonverbal behavior and the vertical dimension of social relations. Psychological Bulletin, 131(6), 898-924 -- a more robust meta-analytic source.
- **✦ Corrections applied**:
    - *replacement_recommended*: Hall, Coats & LeBeau (2005) meta-analysis "Nonverbal behavior and the vertical dimension of social relations" Psychological Bulletin 131:898-924
    - *note*: REPLICATION CONCERN. The narrow "posture signals status" claim the hub makes IS supported, but Carney et al. 2010 power-posing hormonal/risk-tolerance findings have famously failed to replicate. Hall/Coats/LeBeau 2005 supports the same narrow claim without replication baggage.

#### Chapman (2022)

- **Citation**: Chapman and Carel (2022) extend this analysis, arguing that autistic thriving has been "rendered both invisible and unthinkable by interlocking forms of testimonial and hermeneutical injustice." They identify an epistemic catch-22: **one can be recognized as autistic or as thriving, but not both** -- because mainstream conceptions of the good life have excluded autistic manifestations of happiness and flourishing.
- **Links**: [Neurodiversity, epistemic injustice, and the good human life](https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/josp.12456), [Wiley](https://doi.org/10.1111/josp.12456)
- **Cited in**: `category-manipulation-research-practical.md`, `category-manipulation-research-theory.md`
- **Impact**: load-bearing · **Confidence**: high
- **Variants merged**: 2
- **Claim**: In-text description of Chapman & Carel (2022): autistic thriving rendered invisible through interlocking epistemic injustices; one can be recognized as autistic OR thriving, not both. | Full citation of Chapman & Carel 2022.
- **Source**: Chapman & Carel (2022, Journal of Social Philosophy 53(4):614-631) make exactly this argument about autistic thriving and epistemic injustice.
- **Support**: Hub's framing and quote accurately reflect the paper's thesis. | DOI verified.

#### Colich (2012)

- **Citation**: - Colich, N. L., et al. (2012). Individuals with Autism Spectrum Disorders Do Not Use Social Stereotypes in Irony Comprehension. PMC3991690.
- **Links**: [PMC3991690](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC3991690/)
- **Cited in**: `category-11-nonliteral-research-detection.md`
- **Impact**: supporting · **Confidence**: medium
- **Claim**: Cited to support the claim that autistic individuals do not use occupational stereotype information to detect irony / sarcasm in the way NT controls do.
- **Source**: The PMC3991690 paper is actually Zalla, Amsellem, Chaby, Sirigu, & Leboyer (2014), PLOS ONE — not Colich 2012. The paper's findings (stereotype does not spontaneously modulate irony detection in HFA/AS) do match the hub's claim.
- **Support**: The claim is supported by the actual paper at PMC3991690, but the attribution is wrong: author is Zalla et al., year 2014, not Colich 2012.
- **Notes**: CITATION ERROR: The PMC ID points to Zalla et al. (2014) 'Individuals with Autism Spectrum Disorders Do Not Use Social Stereotypes in Irony Comprehension,' PLOS ONE 9(4):e95568. The claim itself is supported but author and year should be corrected to Zalla et al. 2014.
- **✦ Corrections applied**:
    - *replace_with*: Zalla, Amsellem, Chaby, Sirigu & Leboyer (2014)
    - *correct_journal*: PLOS ONE 9(4):e95568
    - *note*: AUTHOR/YEAR MISATTRIBUTION. PMC3991690 is actually Zalla et al. 2014. The claim (autistic individuals do not use social stereotypes in irony comprehension) IS supported by the real paper.

#### Crompton (2020)

- **Citation**: - **Crompton et al. (2020) on autistic peer-to-peer communication** establishes the empirical basis for why autistic peer reality-check is particularly useful for this pattern: autistic peers can often recognize the credentials/behavior gap more quickly than allistic friends can, because they have usually lived through it and because they do not process the credentials through the cultural script of *allies as the good guys.*

#### Debrabander (2019)

- **Citation**: DeBrabander et al.'s (2019) thin-slice work ran the study in the opposite direction: when autistic raters evaluated thin-slice videos of autistic speakers, they did not show the negative first-impression bias that non-autistic raters showed. The autistic raters were rating the same speakers more accurately, or at least more charitably, because they were not bringing the non-autistic frame-repertoire to the task.
- **Links**: [PMC](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC8992867/), [doi.org](https://doi.org/10.1089/aut.2019.0018)
- **Cited in**: `category-23-framing-research-autism.md`, `category-24-identity-stance-research-autism.md`, `tier-1-double-empathy.md`
- **Impact**: load-bearing · **Confidence**: high
- **Variants merged**: 2
- **Claim**: Cited as the thin-slice study showing that autistic raters do not show the negative first-impression bias toward autistic speakers that non-autistic raters show. | In-text narrative version of the same DeBrabander 2019 finding.
- **Source**: Same source.
- **Support**: Directly supports the rater-neurotype claim. | Directly supports the claim.

#### Depraetere (2023)

- **Citation**: Depraetere and Kaltenbock (2023) identify two main functions for hedged performatives: **downtoner** (reducing force) and **emphasizer** (adding force through apparent hedging). They play an important role in rapport management, serving mainly positive face politeness strategies.
- **Links**: [SAGE Journals](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/full/10.1177/00754242231173419)
- **Cited in**: `category-06-hedging-research-theory.md`
- **Impact**: supporting · **Confidence**: high
- **Variants merged**: 2
- **Claim**: Cited for the analysis of hedged performatives as having downtoner vs. emphasizer functions in American English. | In-text narrative of the same Depraetere & Kaltenböck 2023 claim.
- **Source**: Same source.
- **Support**: Directly supports the hedged-performatives claim. | Directly supports the claim.
- **Notes**: Journal title may actually be 'Journal of English Linguistics' rather than 'Language in Society' — DOI 00754242 is the JEL prefix, not Language in Society. Minor metadata issue.
- **✦ Corrections applied**:
    - *correct_journal*: Journal of English Linguistics
    - *note*: JOURNAL ERROR. Hub misattributed to Language in Society; DOI prefix matches JEL.

#### Diehl (2009)

- **Citation**: - **Diehl et al. (2009)** conducted acoustic analysis of prosody in high-functioning autistic speakers and found pitch variation differences, with autistic speakers showing broader pitch range in some contexts and flatter contours in others. The key finding was that the differences were real but heterogeneous — no single "autistic prosody" profile, but a statistically distinguishable pattern. [VALIDATED]
- **Cited in**: `manipulation-hub-pattern-emotional-invalidation-double-bind.md`, `manipulation-hub-pattern-pragmatic-packaging-invalidation.md`, `manipulation-hub-pattern-tone-policing.md`
- **Impact**: supporting · **Confidence**: high
- **Variants merged**: 2
- **Claim**: Cited as the acoustic analysis of HFA prosody finding real but heterogeneous pitch-range differences. | In-text description of the Diehl 2009 prosody finding.
- **Source**: Same source.
- **Support**: Directly supports the heterogeneous-prosody claim. | Directly supports the claim.

#### Douglas (2024)

- **Citation**: Douglas & Sedgewick (2024) introduced the *weaponized features* framing as a conceptual contribution from autistic-led qualitative research. The argument: perpetrators of abuse against autistic people often appear to **deliberately** exploit autism-related features as components of their tactics. The features are not the cause of the abuse; the *exploitation* of the features is the tactic.
- **Links**: [PMC11191373](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC11191373/), [DOI](https://doi.org/10.1177/13623613231205630), [SAGE](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/10.1177/13623613231205630)
- **Cited in**: 42 files incl. `category-21-contexts-research-autism.md`, `category-23-framing-frame-analysis.md`, …
- **Impact**: load-bearing · **Confidence**: high
- **Variants merged**: 7
- **Claim**: Short-form citation of the Douglas & Sedgewick 2024 weaponized-features qualitative study. | Cites Douglas & Sedgewick 2024 with specific prevalence percentages for DV, physical violence, sexual violence, and stalking. | Primary load-bearing citation for the manipulation hub: the weaponized-features framework and autistic interpersonal-victimization themes.
- **Source**: Same source.
- **Support**: Directly supports the hub's weaponized-features framing. | The general claim is supported, but the specific percentages cited (23.7%, 56-58.5%, etc.) appear to be from studies cited within the Douglas & Sedgewick review, not primary findings of the study itself. | Primary source for the weaponized-features framework; directly supports the core manipulation-hub framing.
- **Notes**: Prevalence percentages attributed to Douglas & Sedgewick may actually trace to other studies the paper reviews (e.g., Griffiths et al., Pecora et al.). Verify primary attribution before press. | Duplicate variant (heavy repetition across manipulation hub files).

#### Drew (1992)

- **Support**: Drew & Heritage 1992 is the canonical definition of institutional talk and directly underwrites the hub's claim that topic/turn rules vary by institutional context.

#### Drew (1997)

#### Frazier (2009)

- **Citation**: - **Frazier et al. (2009)** critique: much of the PTG literature relies on retrospective self-report, which has known biases. When growth is measured prospectively (comparing before-trauma and after-trauma measures directly), the effect is much smaller than retrospective studies suggest. Some of what people report as "growth" may be reframing of how they remember their pre-trauma selves, not actual change.
- **Cited in**: `manipulation-hub-strategy-recovery.md`
- **Impact**: load-bearing · **Confidence**: high
- **Variants merged**: 2
- **Claim**: In-text attribution for the critique of retrospective self-report bias in PTG research. | Full bibliographic entry for the Frazier 2009 PTG critique.
- **Source**: Frazier et al. 2009 Psychological Science 20(7):912-919 found that prospective pre-post measures of growth showed much smaller effects than retrospective self-reports, suggesting PTG as commonly measured is inflated by reframing.
- **Support**: Exact match — canonical critique of PTG retrospective measurement. | Exact match.

- **Links**: [Wikisource (English)](https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/On_Sense_and_Reference)

- **Claim**: Cited as the origin of presupposition theory — Frege's observation that definite descriptions presuppose (not assert) referent existence.

#### Garfinkel (2016)

- **Cited in**: `category-24-identity-stance-research-practical.md`, `manipulation-hub-context-medical-settings.md`, `manipulation-hub-pattern-emotional-invalidation-double-bind.md`, `manipulation-hub-pattern-sensory-gaslighting.md`

#### Gunraj (2016)

- **Citation**: **Gunraj et al. (2016)** conducted experiments in which participants ranked the sincerity of text messages and handwritten letters that included final periods. Participants ranked text messages ending with a period as less sincere — but handwritten notes ending with a period as more sincere. This demonstrates that the pragmatic value of punctuation is **medium-specific**: the same mark carries different meanings in different contexts.
- **Links**: [ScienceDirect (paywalled)](https://doi.org/10.1016/j.chb.2015.11.003)
- **Cited in**: `category-10-relevance-theory-research-practical.md`, `category-20-digital-pragmatics-research-practical.md`, `category-20-digital-pragmatics-research-theory.md`
- **Impact**: supporting · **Confidence**: high
- **Variants merged**: 2
- **Claim**: Empirical anchor for the claim that period-final punctuation is perceived as less sincere in text messages, demonstrating medium-specific pragmatics. | Full-form citation for the period-insincerity effect in text messaging.
- **Source**: Gunraj, Drumm-Hewitt, Dashow, Upadhyay & Klin (2016) Computers in Human Behavior 55:1067-1075: text-message responses ending with a period were rated as LESS sincere than those without; for handwritten notes, NO such effect was found.
- **Support**: Partially — the text-message finding is accurate; the hub's claim that handwritten notes ending with a period were rated 'more sincere' overstates the result, which was a null effect (no difference), not a reversal. | Directly — verified bibliographic record.
- **Notes**: Minor mischaracterization: Gunraj found text-period decreased sincerity but handwritten-period produced NO difference, not an increase. Suggest rewording: 'text periods decreased sincerity while handwritten periods had no such effect.'
- **✦ Corrections applied**:
    - *note*: FINDING MISCHARACTERIZATION. Hub says handwritten notes ending in period were rated "more sincere" — actual finding is NO EFFECT for handwritten (only text messages showed the effect). Rewording required.

#### Hull (2019)

- **Citation**: **Hull et al. (2017, PMC5509825)** introduced the CAT-Q (Camouflaging Autistic Traits Questionnaire) and documented that masking — the conscious modulation of communication and affect to appear less autistic — imposes substantial cognitive and emotional costs. **Hull et al. (2019)** deepened this with qualitative work on the reasons, contexts, and costs of camouflaging. **Cook et al. (2021)** conducted a systematic review that found camouflaging to be consistently associated with anxiety, depression, burnout, and the erosion of self-knowledge. [VALIDATED — one of the strongest consensus findings in contemporary autism research]
- **Links**: [PMC5509825](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC5509825/), [Hull et al. (2019) - Development and Validation of the CAT-Q](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6394586/), [PMC](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC6394785/), [Springer](https://doi.org/10.1007/s10803-018-3792-6), [Springer](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10803-018-03878-x)
- **Cited in**: 7 files incl. `category-07-register-research-autism.md`, `category-07-register.md`, …
- **Impact**: load-bearing · **Confidence**: high
- **Variants merged**: 9
- **Claim**: Summary statement framing Hull 2019 as a qualitative deepening of the 2017 Hull paper — reasons, contexts, and costs of camouflaging. | Cited as the paper that developed and validated the CAT-Q — the standard self-report camouflaging measure. | Short-form citation for the CAT-Q development paper.
- **Source**: Hull et al. (2019) "Understanding the Reasons, Contexts and Costs of Camouflaging" (JADD 49:1899-1911) is a thematic analysis of 262 autistic adults describing why they camouflage, in what situations, and at what cost.
- **Support**: Direct: the 2019 paper explicitly frames itself as examining reasons/contexts/costs, exactly as the hub summarizes. | Direct and canonical: the paper's title and purpose are exactly the CAT-Q development and validation the hub cites. | Direct: the CAT-Q is exactly what the paper develops.
- **Notes**: Minor historical error in the hub's passage: CAT-Q was actually introduced in Hull et al. 2019, not 2017 — the 2017 paper is the qualitative precursor. The claim sentence should swap these. | Minor: the first URL (PMC6394586) points to a different article; the correct PMC ID for Hull 2019 CAT-Q is PMC6394785. Housekeeping fix needed. | Year error: "Is social camouflaging associated with anxiety and depression..." (Molecular Autism) is a 2021 paper, not 2019. | Housekeeping: duplicate bare-citation variant. | Year error: actual publication is 2020, not 2019.

### I

#### Ide (1989)

- **Citation**: - Ide, S. (1989). "Formal Forms and Discernment: Two Neglected Aspects of Universals of Linguistic Politeness." *Multilingua*, 8(2-3), 223-248. [De Gruyter (paywalled)](https://doi.org/10.1515/mult.1989.8.2-3.223)

- **Cited in**: `category-03-politeness-research-offrecord-criticisms.md`
- **Impact**: supporting · **Confidence**: high
- **Claim**: Cited as a canonical critique of Brown & Levinson politeness theory that introduced the discernment/wakimae concept for Japanese.
- **Source**: Ide (1989) argues that Brown & Levinson's strategic volitional model misses a second dimension, discernment (wakimae), where speakers select linguistically-obligatory forms (e.g., Japanese honorifics) based on social role.
- **Support**: Direct; this is exactly the Ide critique the hub references.

#### Inoue (2023)

- **Citation**: - Inoue, K. et al. (2023). "Autistic Traits Associated with Dichotomous Thinking Mediated by Intolerance of Uncertainty." *Scientific Reports*. [Nature](https://www.nature.com/articles/s41598-023-41164-8)

- **Claim**: Cited for the finding that autistic traits are associated with dichotomous (black-and-white) thinking, mediated by intolerance of uncertainty — used to explain false-dilemma vulnerability.
- **Source**: Inoue et al. (2023, Scientific Reports) report a mediation analysis in which autistic traits predict dichotomous thinking with intolerance of uncertainty as a mediator.
- **Support**: Direct — the paper's title and core result exactly match the hub's claim.

#### Irvine (2016)

- **Citation**: - Irvine, C.A., Eigsti, I.-M., & Fein, D.A. (2016). "Uh, Um, and Autism: Filler Disfluencies as Pragmatic Markers in Adolescents with Optimal Outcomes from Autism Spectrum Disorder." *J Autism Dev Disord*, 46(3), 1061-1070. [PMC7536647](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC7536647/) *(corrects earlier misattribution to "Lake et al. 2011")*
- **Links**: [Irvine, Eigsti & Fein (2016) - "Uh, Um, and Autism: Filler Disfluencies as Pragmatic Markers in Adolescents with Optimal Outcomes from Autism Spectrum Disorder"](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC7536647/)

- **Claim**: Cited for the finding that autistic adolescents produce 'um' less frequently than TD peers or optimal-outcome peers, with um-rate associated with autism severity.

#### Isaacs (1990)

- **Citation**: - Isaacs, E.A. & Clark, H.H. (1990). "Ostensible invitations." *Language in Society*, 19, 493-509. (Research on sincere vs. insincere invitations) [Cambridge (paywalled)](https://doi.org/10.1017/S0047404500014780)
- **Links**: [Cambridge (paywalled)](https://doi.org/10.1017/S0047404500014780)
- **Cited in**: `category-08-deixis-research-everyday.md`
- **Impact**: supporting · **Confidence**: high
- **Claim**: Cited as the canonical source for the sincere vs. ostensible (pro forma) invitation distinction.
- **Source**: Isaacs & Clark (1990) show that speakers produce and hearers detect 'ostensible' invitations — invitations uttered without sincere intent to host — via recognizable cues and conventions.
- **Support**: Direct; the paper's topic is exactly the sincere/insincere invitation distinction the hub references.

#### Iyengar (1991)

- **Citation**: - Iyengar, S. (1991). *Is Anyone Responsible? How Television Frames Political Issues*. University of Chicago Press. [Amazon](https://www.amazon.com/Anyone-Responsible-Television-Political-American/dp/0226388557)
- **Links**: [Amazon](https://www.amazon.com/Anyone-Responsible-Television-Political-American/dp/0226388557)

- **Impact**: load-bearing · **Confidence**: high
- **Claim**: Cited as the foundational source for the episodic vs. thematic news-frame distinction and its attribution-of-responsibility effects.
- **Source**: Iyengar 1991 introduces the episodic/thematic frame distinction in television news and demonstrates experimentally that episodic frames shift attribution from societal to individual causes.
- **Support**: Direct and canonical for media-framing theory.

#### Izuma (2011)

- **Citation**: 2. Izuma, K., Matsumoto, K., Camerer, C.F., & Adolphs, R. (2011). "Insensitivity to social reputation in autism." *PNAS*, 108(42), 17302-17307. https://www.pnas.org/doi/10.1073/pnas.1107038108
- **Links**: [pnas.org](https://www.pnas.org/doi/10.1073/pnas.1107038108)
- **Cited in**: `category-22-hierarchy-research-autism.md`
- **Impact**: load-bearing · **Confidence**: high
- **Claim**: Cited for the finding that autistic adults do not increase prosocial donations in the presence of an observer, unlike neurotypical controls — a behavioural index of reduced reputation management.
- **Source**: Izuma et al. (2011, PNAS 108:17302-17307) found that high-functioning autistic adults donated the same amount in observed vs. unobserved conditions, whereas matched controls donated significantly more when observed.
- **Support**: Direct; this is the paper's headline result.

#### Kahneman (1979)

- **Claim**: Cited as the origin of prospect theory, the framework underlying gain/loss framing effects.

#### Kahneman (1981)

- **Citation**: - Kahneman, D., & Tversky, A. (1981). The framing of decisions and the psychology of choice. *Science*, 211(4481), 453-458. [Science (paywalled)](https://doi.org/10.1126/science.7455683)

- **Claim**: Cited as the canonical demonstration of framing effects on decisions (Asian disease problem) and the foundation for behavioural-economic framing research.
- **Source**: Tversky & Kahneman 1981 Science is the 'Asian disease' paper showing that gain vs. loss framings of identical outcomes produce opposite preferences.
- **Support**: Direct and canonical.

- **Cited in**: `category-15-prosodic-pragmatics-research-examples.md`
- **Impact**: supporting · **Confidence**: high
- **Claim**: Cited as empirical evidence that contrastive focus and discourse-new focus are phonetically distinguishable in English.
- **Source**: Katz & Selkirk (2011, Language 87:771-816) measured phonetic prominence in English and found contrastive-focus items show greater duration, intensity, and f0 than discourse-new items.
- **Support**: Direct and canonical for the contrastive/discourse-new phonetic distinction.
- **✦ Corrections applied**:
    - *correct_author_order*: Katz & Selkirk
    - *note*: AUTHOR ORDER. Hub had Selkirk & Katz.

#### Keating (2021)

- **Source**: Keating & Cook (2021, Autism Research) review evidence that autistic and NT individuals each struggle to read the other's facial expressions, supporting a mismatch (not deficit) account.

#### Keysar (1998)

#### Keysar (2000)

- **Claim**: Cited as the canonical experimental demonstration that listeners initially process referential expressions egocentrically before using common ground.
- **Source**: Keysar, Barr, Balin & Brauner (2000, Psych Science 11:32-38) used eye-tracking in a Director task and showed listeners frequently consider privileged-ground referents before adjusting toward mutual knowledge.

#### Kotthoff (1993)

- **Citation**: - Kotthoff, H. (1993). Disagreement and concession in disputes: On the context sensitivity of preference structures. *Language in Society*, 22(2), 193-216. [CUP (paywalled)](https://doi.org/10.1017/S0047404500017103)
- **Links**: [CUP (paywalled)](https://doi.org/10.1017/S0047404500017103)
- **Cited in**: `category-18-refusal-research-theory.md`
- **Impact**: supporting · **Confidence**: high
- **Claim**: Cited as evidence that preference structures (dispreferred status of disagreement) are context-sensitive — in dispute contexts disagreement can become preferred.
- **Source**: Kotthoff 1993 Language in Society 22:193-216 argues that in argumentative contexts disagreement loses its dispreferred character and concessions become the marked form, inverting standard preference structure.
- **Support**: Direct and canonical.

#### Krifka (2008)

- **Claim**: Supports dissociation: ToM predicts scalar but not ad-hoc implicature performance in autistic children. | Same ToM/scalar/ad-hoc dissociation claim. | Supports claim that conventionalized indirect requests are processed well by autistic adults but hints are harder.
- **Source**: Verified: Mazzaggio, Foppolo, Job & Surian (2021) J. Communication Disorders 90. 26 ASD + 26 TD children; ToM linked to scalar but not ad-hoc.
- **Support**: Directly supports the dissociation claim. | Source explicitly states this dissociation. | Directly supports the conventionalized-vs-hint dissociation.
- **Notes**: Lead author is Marocchini, not Mazzaggio. 'Mazzaggio et al.' is not standard citation practice; first-author citation (Marocchini et al.) would be more accurate. Note: Slices 1-7 flagged the same PMC as 'actually Marocchini et al.' — content claim still stands.

#### Nadig (2009)

- **Citation**: - Nadig, A., Vivanti, G. & Ozonoff, S. (2009). [The art of common ground: emergence of a complex pragmatic language skill in adolescents with ASD](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC4764348/). -- Common ground and referential shortening study
- **Links**: [Nadig, Vivanti & Ozonoff (2009)](https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/19998354/), [The art of common ground](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC4764348/)
- **Cited in**: `category-16-common-ground-research-autism.md`, `category-info-structure-research-autism.md`
- **Impact**: load-bearing · **Confidence**: high
- **Variants merged**: 2
- **Claim**: Supports common-ground / audience design claims in ASD. | Hub attributes 'The art of common ground' paper to Nadig/Vivanti/Ozonoff (2009) via PMC4764348.
- **Source**: CRITICAL MISATTRIBUTION: PMC4764348 is actually 'The art of common ground: emergence of a complex pragmatic language skill in adolescents with autism spectrum disorders' by Ashley de Marchena and Inge-Marie Eigsti (2015) in Journal of Child Language — NOT Nadig/Vivanti/Ozonoff 2009.
- **Support**: Directly supports audience-design / common-ground claims. | Content claim about common ground IS supported by PMC4764348, but the authorship and year are wrong. Nadig, Vivanti & Ozonoff (2009) is a real but DIFFERENT paper (Autism Research, object-description adaptation).
- **Notes**: AUTHOR/TITLE MISATTRIBUTION — matches the known error mode (slices 1-7 found the same PMC4764348 mis-attributed to Hyter 2017). The real authors of 'The art of common ground' paper are de Marchena & Eigsti (2015). Hub is conflating two papers.
- **✦ Corrections applied**:
    - *note*: PMC CONFLATION. PMC4764348 previously (wrongly) cited here is actually de Marchena & Eigsti 2015 (same bad lookup as Hyter 2017 entry). Real Nadig/Vivanti/Ozonoff 2009 is a different paper: object-description adaptation, PubMed 19998354. Fix: Nadig entry gets the real 2009 paper; PMC4764348 routes to de Marchena entry.

#### Pearson (2023)

- **Citation**: Pearson, Rose & Rees (2023) titled their qualitative study "'I felt like I deserved it because I was autistic'" because that phrase, or close variants, recurred across their interview participants. The phrase is the endpoint of a successful internalization: the autistic person no longer needs the abuser to discount them, because they are doing it themselves. The diagnostic frame has become the operating premise.
- **Links**: [Sage](https://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/13623613221104546)
- **Cited in**: 29 files incl. `category-21-contexts-research-autism.md`, `category-24-identity-stance-research-autism.md`, …
- **Impact**: load-bearing · **Confidence**: high
- **Variants merged**: 6
- **Claim**: Used as the source for the claim that autistic survivors in the Pearson sample had few trusted others to reality-check against. | Cited throughout the manipulation hub as the core qualitative evidence for internalization of blame after interpersonal victimization. | Uses Pearson et al. 2023 internalization findings to support the claim that allyship-adjacent features help internalization stick.
- **Source**: The paper's title does come from recurring participant phrasing — this is accurate.
- **Support**: Supports the reality-check-network claim; consistent with the qualitative findings. | Directly supports internalization / self-blame framing. | Broad framing is supported. The specific 'allyship-adjacent features' framing is an interpretive extension by the hub author of the qualitative data.
- **Notes**: The Pearson paper does not explicitly theorize 'allyship-adjacent features' — this is the hub's framing layered on top of the qualitative findings. | Housekeeping duplicate. | Housekeeping duplicate of the full bibliographic entry.

#### Pijnacker (2009)

- **Citation**: - **Pijnacker et al. (2009)** tested 11 adults with autistic disorder, 17 with Asperger syndrome, and 28 controls. The combined ASD group was **just as likely as controls to derive scalar implicatures**. However, there was a difference between the autistic disorder and Asperger syndrome subgroups, suggesting heterogeneity within the spectrum. Published in *Journal of Autism and Developmental Disorders*, 39(4), 607-618. PubMed ID: 19052858
- **Links**: [PubMed 19063905](https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/19063905/), [Link](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s10803-008-0661-8), [Springer (paywalled)](https://doi.org/10.1007/s10803-008-0661-8)
- **Cited in**: `category-02-implicature-research-autism.md`, `category-02-implicature.md`, `category-10-relevance-theory-research-autism.md`, `category-fallacies-research-autism.md`, `tier-1-cooperative-principle.md`
- **Impact**: load-bearing · **Confidence**: high
- **Variants merged**: 5
- **Claim**: Cited as evidence that autistic adults engage in defeasible reasoning. | Cites Pijnacker et al. 2009 as evidence for scalar-implicature performance in 28 autistic adults. | Full bibliographic form of the canonical Pijnacker et al. 2009 JADD paper.
- **Source**: All details (N, subgroup heterogeneity, issue info, PubMed ID) are correct.
- **Support**: Directly supports the defeasible-reasoning framing. | Accurate summary. | Direct attribution.
- **Notes**: Housekeeping duplicate.

#### Shield (2015)

- **Citation**: - [Shield, Meier & Tager-Flusberg (2015) The Use of Sign Language Pronouns by Native-Signing Children with Autism (PMC4474758)](https://pmc.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/articles/PMC4474758/)

- **Source**: Verified: Shield, Meier & Tager-Flusberg (2015), JADD 45(7):2128-2145, DOI 10.1007/s10803-015-2377-x, PMC4474758. The paper's main finding is that signing children with ASD AVOID pronouns in favor of names, revealing that transparency of sign pronouns does NOT eliminate the difficulty — supporting a self/other-perspective hypothesis rather than an opacity hypothesis.
- **Support**: The 2015 paper supports the broader 'specific perspective cost' framing but the palm-orientation-reversal evidence is actually from the follow-up fingerspelling paper (PMC9614108), not the 2015 paper. The hub conflates the two papers slightly, though it does cite the fingerspelling paper separately.
- **Notes**: NUANCE: 2015 paper's key finding is pronoun AVOIDANCE (not reversal). Palm-orientation reversal evidence is in the fingerspelling follow-up (PMC9614108). Hub should be precise about which finding is from which paper.
- **✦ Corrections applied**:
    - *note*: FINDING BLUR. 2015 Shield/Meier/Tager-Flusberg paper's key finding is that signing autistic children AVOID pronouns (in favor of names), not palm-orientation reversals. Palm reversal is the 2023 fingerspelling follow-up PMC9614108 (cited separately). Deixis chapter blurs which finding comes from which paper.
